Funeral Procedures: Walking, Grave, and Placement Rulings
Commentary expanding funeral rulings with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the preferred manner of walking in a funeral procession, explain why lahd is preferred over shaq, list the mustahabb and wajib actions when placing the deceased in the grave, and identify the basis for covering a female's grave.

Core Idea
Walking behind the bier is sunnah, lahd is best, and covering a female's grave is mustahabb—all rooted in prophetic practice.

The funeral (janaza) involves a sequence of actions from carrying the bier to closing the grave. The Hanafi school, relying on hadith and the practice of the Companions, has established preferences and prohibitions for each step. This lesson unpacks the commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on the core rulings for walking, grave construction, placement, and covering, helping you distinguish between what is wajib, sunnah, mustahabb, and makruh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
For each action described (walking behind the bier, making a lahd, placing from qibla side, covering the grave for women, using bricks/wood), determine the hukm level: wajib, sunnah muakkadah, mustahabb, or makruh.
The text uses terms like 'ahab' (more beloved) for walking behind, 'afdal' for lahd, 'wajib' for turning toward qibla, and 'yukrah' for bricks/wood.
2
Locate the dalil for each ruling
For the key rulings (walking behind, lahd, entering from qibla side), find the hadith cited by the commentator. Note the source and the narrator chain where mentioned.
The lahd ruling relies on 'al-lahdu lana wa al-shaqqu li-ghayrina'; walking behind on the hadith from 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Abza with 'Ali's explanation.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how each hadith proves the specific ruling. For example, the phrase 'al-lahdu lana' indicates it is our (Muslims') practice, implying preference unless necessity dictates otherwise.
The commentarial tradition often unpacks the linguistic or contextual implications of the proof text.
4
Account for ikhtilaf and exceptions
Although the passage presents a unified Hanafi view, note any differences: some Hanafi sources (al-Muhit) suggest waiting until the grave is leveled before sitting, while others disagree. Also, shaq is permitted in case of necessity.
The commentator weighs the views and states the first view (that of the matn) is preferred (al-awwal awla).
5
Apply to a real burial scenario
Imagine you are attending a janaza. Based on this lesson, identify which actions you should perform correctly: walk behind the bier, ensure the grave is a lahd (if soil permits), enter the deceased from the qibla side, untie the knot, and if the deceased is female, cover the grave with a cloth during burial.
Avoid disliked materials like baked bricks or wood for the grave's inner lining.

And walking behind it is more beloved. And the grave is dug and made a lahd, and he is entered into it from the direction of the qibla. ... [1] His statement: 'and walking behind it' i.e. behind the funeral, 'more beloved' i.e. better and more virtuous, even though walking in front, to the right, and to the left is permissible, due to the hadeeth of 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Abza... [2] His statement: 'and he makes a lahd' i.e. makes a lahd, due to the hadeeth: 'The lahd is for us and the shaq is for others'... [3] His statement: 'from what is adjacent' i.e. he takes the deceased into the grave from the direction of the qibla, and this is reported from the Messenger of Allah when burying some of his Companions... [following pages] And he says: 'In the name of Allah and upon the religion of the Messenger of Allah' and he is turned toward the qibla, and the knot is untied... And the clay and reeds are leveled, and her grave is covered with a cloth, and bricks and wood are disliked, and the soil is thrown upon it.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary (sharh) on Sharh al-Wiqayah, expanding on funeral rulings (ahkam al-jana'iz). It covers several sequential actions in the funeral process with their preferred and disliked aspects. The passage begins by stating that walking behind the funeral bier is more beloved (ahab) than walking in front, to the right, or to the left, though those positions are permissible. The commentator cites a hadith from 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Abza where 'Ali (RA) explains that the virtue of walking behind is like the virtue of congregational prayer over individual prayer, but Abu Bakr and 'Umar walked in front to make it easy for people. This shows the ruling is recommendatory (mustahabb), not obligatory. The next ruling concerns digging the grave: it should be made a lahd (a niche on the qibla side of the grave) rather than a shaq (a trench in the middle), based on the hadith 'al-lahdu lana wa al-shaqqu li-ghayrina' (the lahd is for us and the shaq for others). The lahd is preferred unless necessity requires the shaq. Then, when placing the deceased in the grave, one should enter from the direction of the qibla, and this was the practice of the Prophet (SAW). The commentator also notes that the deceased should be turned toward the qibla, preferably on the right side (as mentioned in al-Durr al-Mukhtar), and this is wajib. The knot of the shroud is untied to prevent the shroud from spreading and to facilitate the deceased sitting up during questioning by the angels. The grave is leveled with clay (liyyin) and reeds (qasab) to seal gaps and prevent soil from falling on the deceased. For a female deceased, the grave is covered with a cloth during burial out of modesty (this is mustahabb for women, not men). Baked bricks (ajar) and wood (khashab) are disliked (makruh) because they resemble permanent construction and, in the case of bricks, have traces of fire (an association with Hellfire, hence a bad omen). The soil is thrown in after burial. The commentator references other works like 'al-Khaniyya', 'al-'Inayah', 'al-Muhit', 'Bahr al-Ra'iq', 'Halyat al-Majli', 'Sharh al-Munyah', and 'al-Durr al-Mukhtar'.

Key Terms
اللحد (al-lahd): A niche dug in the side of the grave on the qibla side, in which the deceased is placed.
الشق (al-shaq): A trench dug in the middle of the grave floor, as opposed to a niche on the side.
التسجية (al-tasjiyah): Covering the grave with a cloth, specifically for female deceased during burial.
الآجر (al-ajar): Baked bricks, used in construction.
الكفن (al-kafan): The shroud in which the deceased is wrapped.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in this passage, what is the ruling for making a lahd (niche) in the grave?
A) It is wajib and substituting it with shaq is invalid.
B) It is afdal (preferred) and shaq is permissible only in necessity.
C) It is haram to use shaq.
D) There is no preference between lahd and shaq.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi funeral rites emphasize following the prophetic model: walking behind the bier, using a lahd grave, turning the deceased toward the qibla, and observing modesty for female deceased, while avoiding innovations like baked bricks.
